In an era before computerized databases, organized case law, and treatises,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 16" name="FRconcurrence1.16" href="#FNconcurrence1.16">16</a></sup> the American drafters probably would have known about particular patents only if they were well publicized or subject to reported litigation.  So far as I am aware, no published cases pertained to patents on business methods.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Also noteworthy is what was <em>not </em>patented under the English system.  During the 17th and 18th centuries, Great Britain saw innovations in business organization,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 17" name="FRconcurrence1.17" href="#FNconcurrence1.17">17</a></sup> business models,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 18" name="FRconcurrence1.18" href="#FNconcurrence1.18">18</a></sup> management techniques,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 19" name="FRconcurrence1.19" href="#FNconcurrence1.19">19</a></sup> and novel solutions to the challenges of operating global firms in which subordinate managers could be reached only by a long sea voyage.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 20" name="FRconcurrence1.20" href="#FNconcurrence1.20">20</a></sup>  Few if any of these methods of conducting business were patented.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 21" name="FRconcurrence1.21" href="#FNconcurrence1.21">21</a></sup></p>
<p>

<em>Early American Patent Law</em></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;At the Constitutional Convention, the Founders decided to give Congress a patent power so that it might "promote the Progress of ... useful Arts."  Art.&nbsp;I, §8, cl.&nbsp;8.  There is little known history of that Clause.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 22" name="FRconcurrence1.22" href="#FNconcurrence1.22">22</a></sup>  We do know that the Clause passed without objection or debate.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 23" name="FRconcurrence1.23" href="#FNconcurrence1.23">23</a></sup>  This is striking because other proposed powers, such as a power to grant charters of incorporation, generated discussion about the fear that they might breed "monopolies."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 24" name="FRconcurrence1.24" href="#FNconcurrence1.24">24</a></sup>  Indeed, at the ratification conventions, some States recommended amendments that would have prohibited Congress from granting "&nbsp;'exclusive advantages of commerce.'&nbsp;"&nbsp;<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 25" name="FRconcurrence1.25" href="#FNconcurrence1.25">25</a></sup>  If the original understanding of the Patent Clause included the authority to patent methods of doing business, it might not have passed so quietly.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 1790, Congress passed the first Patent Act, an "Act to promote the progress of useful Arts" that authorized patents for persons who had "invented or discovered any useful art, manufacture, engine, machine, or device, or any improvement therein not before known or used," if "the invention or discovery [was] sufficiently useful and important."  1 Stat. 109-110.  Three years later, Congress passed the Patent Act of 1793 and slightly modified the language to cover "any new and useful art, machine, manufacture or composition of matter, or any new and useful improvement on any art, machine, manufacture or composition of matter."  1 Stat. 319.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The object of the constitutional patent power and the statutory authorization for process patents in the early patent Acts was the term "useful art."  It is not evident from the face of the statutes or the Constitution whether the objects of the patent system were "arts" that are also useful, or rather a more specific category, the class of arts known as "useful arts."  Cf. <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 12 (describing the "&nbsp;'new and useful' tests which have always existed in the statutory scheme" and apply to all categories of subject matter).  However, we have generally assumed that "useful art," at least as it is used in the Patent Act, is itself a term of art.  See <em>Burden</em>, 15 How., at 267-268.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The word "art" and the phrase "useful arts" are subject to many meanings.  There is room on the margins to debate exactly what qualifies as either.  There is room, moreover, to debate at what level of generality we should understand these broad and historical terms, given that "[a] rule that unanticipated inventions are without protection would conflict with the core concept of the patent law," <em>Chakrabarty</em>, 447 U.&nbsp;S., at 316.  It appears, however, that regardless of how one construes the term "useful arts," business methods are not included.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Noah Webster's first American dictionary<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 26" name="FRconcurrence1.26" href="#FNconcurrence1.26">26</a></sup> defined the term "art" as the "disposition or modification of <em>things</em> by human skill, to answer the purpose intended," and differentiated between "useful or mechanic" arts, on the one hand, and "liberal or polite" arts, on the other.  1 An American Dictionary of the English Language (1828) (facsimile edition) (emphasis added).  Although other dictionaries defined the word "art" more broadly,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 27" name="FRconcurrence1.27" href="#FNconcurrence1.27">27</a></sup> Webster's definition likely conveyed a message similar to the meaning of the word "manufactures" in the earlier English statute.  And we know that the term "useful arts" was used in the founding era to refer to manufacturing and similar applied trades.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 28" name="FRconcurrence1.28" href="#FNconcurrence1.28">28</a></sup>  See Coulter, The Field of the Statutory Useful Arts, 34 J. Pat. Off. Soc. 487, 493-500 (1952); see also Thomas, The Patenting of the Liberal Professions, 40 Boston College L.&nbsp;Rev. 1139, 1164 (1999) ("[The Framers of the Constitution] undoubtedly contemplated the industrial, mechanical and manual arts of the late eighteenth Century, in contrast to the seven 'liberal arts' and the four 'fine arts' of classical learning").  Indeed, just days before the Constitutional Convention, one delegate listed examples of American progress in "manufactures and the useful arts," all of which involved the creation or transformation of physical substances.  See T. Coxe, An Address to an Assembly of the Friends of American Manufactures 17-18 (1787) (listing, <em>inter alia</em>, meal, ships, liquors, potash, gunpowder, paper, starch, articles of iron, stone work, carriages, and harnesses).  Numerous scholars have suggested that the term "useful arts" was widely understood to encompass the fields that we would now describe as relating to technology or "technological arts."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 29" name="FRconcurrence1.29" href="#FNconcurrence1.29">29</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, fields such as business and finance were not generally considered part of the "useful arts" in the founding Era.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, The Federalist No. 8, p. 69 (C. Rossiter ed. 1961) (A. Hamilton) (distinguishing between "the arts of industry, and the science of finance"); 30 The Writings of George Washington 1745-1799, p. 186  (J. Fitzpatrick ed. 1939) (writing in a letter that "our commerce has been considerably curtailed," but "the useful arts have been almost imperceptible pushed to a considerable degree of perfection").  Indeed, the same delegate to the Constitutional Convention who gave an address in which he listed triumphs in the useful arts distinguished between those arts and the conduct of business.  He explained that investors were now attracted to the "manufactures and the useful arts," much as they had long invested in "commerce, navigation, stocks, banks, and insurance companies."  T. Coxe, A Statement of the Arts and Manufactures of the United States of America for the Year 1810, (1814), in 2 American State Papers, Finance 666, 688 (1832).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Some scholars have remarked, as did Thomas Jefferson, that early patent statutes neither included nor reflected any serious debate about the precise scope of patentable subject matter.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 9-10 (discussing Thomas Jefferson's observations).  It has been suggested, however, that "[p]erhaps this was in part a function of an understanding--shared widely among legislators, courts, patent office officials, and inventors--about what patents were meant to protect.  Everyone knew that manufactures and machines were at the core of the patent system."  Merges, Property Rights for Business Concepts and Patent System Reform, 14 Berkeley Tech. L.&nbsp;J. 577, 585 (1999) (hereinafter Merges).  Thus, although certain processes, such as those related to the technology of the time, might have been considered patentable, it is possible that "[a]gainst this background, it would have been seen as absurd for an entrepreneur to file a patent" on methods of conducting business.  <em>Ibid.</em></p>
<p>

Development of American Patent Law</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;During the first years of the patent system, no patents were issued on methods of doing business.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 30" name="FRconcurrence1.30" href="#FNconcurrence1.30">30</a></sup> Indeed, for some time, there were serious doubts as to "the patentability of processes per se," as distinct from the physical end product or the tools used to perform a process.  <em>Id.,</em> at 581-582.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 31" name="FRconcurrence1.31" href="#FNconcurrence1.31">31</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thomas Jefferson was the "&nbsp;'first administrator of our patent system'&nbsp;" and "the author of the 1793 Patent Act."  <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 7.  We have said that his "conclusions as to conditions of patentability ... are worthy of note."  <em>Ibid</em>. at 7.  During his time administering the system, Jefferson "saw clearly the difficulty" of deciding what should be patentable.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 32" name="FRconcurrence1.32" href="#FNconcurrence1.32">32</a></sup>  <em>Id.</em>, at 9.  He drafted the 1793 Act, <em>id.</em>, at 7, and, years later, explained that in that Act "&nbsp;'the whole was turned over to the judiciary, to be matured into a system, under which every one might know when his actions were safe and lawful,'&nbsp;" <em>id.</em>, at 10 (quoting Letter to Issac McPherson, in VI Writings of Thomas Jefferson 181-182 (H. Washington ed. 1861)).  As the Court has explained, "Congress agreed with Jefferson ... that the courts should develop additional conditions for patentability."  <em>Graham,</em> 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 10.  Thus "[a]lthough the Patent Act was amended, revised or codified some 50 times between 1790 and 1950, Congress steered clear" of adding statutory requirements of patentability.  <em>Ibid</em>.  For nearly 160 years, Congress retained the term "useful arts," see, <em>e.g.</em>, Act of July 4, 1836, ch. 357, 5 Stat. 117, leaving "wide latitude for judicial construction ... to keep pace with industrial development," Berman, Method Claims, 17 J. Pat. Off. Soc. 713, 714 (1935) (hereinafter Berman).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Although courts occasionally struggled with defining what was a patentable "art" during those 160 years, they consistently rejected patents on methods of doing business.  The rationales for those decisions sometimes varied.  But there was an overarching theme, at least in dicta: Business methods are not patentable arts.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>United States Credit Sys. Co. </em>v. <em>American Credit Indem. Co.,</em> 53 F. 818, 819 (CC NY 1893) ("method of insuring against loss by bad debts" could not be patented "as an art"); <em>Hotel Security Checking Co.</em> v. <em>Lorraine Co.</em>, 160 F. 467, 469 (CA2 1908) ("A system of transacting business disconnected from the means for carrying out the system is not, within the most liberal interpretation of the term, an art"); <em>Guthrie</em> v. <em>Curlett</em>, 10 F.&nbsp;2d 725, 726 (CA2 1926) (method of abbreviating rail tariff schedules, "if it be novel, is not the kind of art protected by the patent acts"); <em>In&nbsp;re Patton</em>, 127 F.&nbsp;2d 324, 327-328 (CCPA 1942) (holding that novel "&nbsp;'interstate and national fire-fighting system'&nbsp;" was not patentable because, <em>inter alia</em>, "a system of transacting business, apart from the means for carrying out such system is not" an art within the meaning of the patent law, "nor is an abstract idea or theory, regardless of its importance or ... ingenuity"); <em>Loew's Drive-In Theatres, Inc.</em> v. <em>Park-In Theatres, Inc.</em>, 174 F.&nbsp;2d 547, 552 (CA1 1949) ("[A] system for the transaction of business, such, for example, as the cafeteria system for transacting the restaurant business ... however novel, useful, or commercially successful is not patentable apart from the means for making the system practically useful, or carrying it out"); <em>Joseph E. Seagram &amp; Sons, Inc.</em> v. <em>Marzall</em>, 180 F.&nbsp;2d 26, 28 (CADC 1950) (method of focus-group testing for beverages is not patentable subject matter); see also <em>In&nbsp;re Howard</em>, 394 F.&nbsp;2d 869, 872 (CCPA 1968) (Kirkpatrick, J., concurring) (explaining that a "method of doing business" cannot be patented).  Between 1790 and 1952, this Court never addressed the patentability of business methods.  But we consistently focused the inquiry on whether an "art" was connected to a machine or physical transformation,<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 33" name="FRconcurrence1.33" href="#FNconcurrence1.33">33</a></sup> an inquiry that would have excluded methods of doing business.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By the early 20th century, it was widely understood that a series of steps for conducting business could not be patented.  A leading treatise, for example, listed "&nbsp;'systems' of business" as an "unpatentable subjec[t]."  1 A. Deller, Walker on Patents §18, p. 62 (1937).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 34" name="FRconcurrence1.34" href="#FNconcurrence1.34">34</a></sup>  Citing many of the cases listed above, the treatise concluded that a "method of transacting business" is not an "&nbsp;'art.'&nbsp;"  <em>Id.</em>, §22, at 69; see also L. Amdur, Patent Law and Practice §39, p. 53 (1935) (listing "Methods of doing business" as an "Unpatentable [A]r[t]"); Berman 718 ("[C]ases have been fairly unanimous in denying patentability to such methods"); Tew, Method of Doing Business, 16 J. Pat. Off. Soc. 607 (1934) ("It is probably settled by long practice and many precedents that 'methods of doing business,' as these words are generally understood, are unpatentable").  Indeed, "[u]ntil recently" it was still "considered well established that [business] methods were non-statutory."  1 R. Moy, Walker on Patents §5:28, p.&nbsp;5-104 (4th ed. 2009).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 35" name="FRconcurrence1.35" href="#FNconcurrence1.35">35</a></sup></p>
<p>

<em>Modern American Patent Law</em></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;By the mid-1900's, many courts were construing the term "art" by using words such as "method, process, system, or like terms."  Berman 713; see <em>Expanded Metal Co.</em> v. <em>Bradford</em>, 214 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 382 (1909) ("The word 'process' has been brought into the decisions because it is supposedly an equivalent form of expression or included in the statutory designation of a new and useful art").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 36" name="FRconcurrence1.36" href="#FNconcurrence1.36">36</a></sup>  Thus in 1952, when Congress updated the patent laws as part of its ongoing project to revise the United States Code, it changed the operative language in §101, replacing the term "art" with "process" and adding a definition of "process" as a "process, art or method," §100(b).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;That change was made for clarity and did not alter the scope of a patentable "process."  See <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 184.  The new terminology was added only in recognition of the fact that courts had been interpreting the category "art" by using the terms "process or method"; Congress thus wanted to avoid "the necessity of explanation that the word 'art' as used in this place means 'process or method.'&nbsp;"  S.&nbsp;Rep. No. 1979, 82d Cong., 2d Sess., 5 (1952) (hereinafter S.&nbsp;Rep. 1979); accord, H.&nbsp;R. Rep. No. 1923, 82d Cong., 2d Sess., 6 (1952) (hereinafter H.&nbsp;R. Rep. 1923); see also <em>id.,</em> at 17 (explaining that "the word 'art'&nbsp;" in §101 "has been interpreted by the courts as being practically synonymous with process or method," and that the switch to the word "[p]rocess" was intended only for clarity).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 37" name="FRconcurrence1.37" href="#FNconcurrence1.37">37</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It appears that when Congress changed the language in §101 to incorporate the prevailing judicial terminology, it merely codified the prevailing judicial interpretation of that category of subject matter.  See <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 184; see also <em>Barber</em> v. <em>Gonzales</em>, 347 U.&nbsp;S. 637, 641 (1954) ("While it is true that statutory language should be interpreted whenever possible according to common usage, some terms acquire a special technical meaning by a process of judicial construction").  Both the Senate and House Committee Reports explained that the word "process" was used in §101 "to clarify the present law as to the patentability of certain types of processes or methods as to which some insubstantial doubts have been expressed."  S.&nbsp;Rep. 1979, at 5; accord, H. Rep. 1923, at 6.  And both noted that those terms were used to convey the prevailing meaning of the term "art," "as interpreted" by courts, S.&nbsp;Rep. 1979, at 17; accord, H. Rep. 1923, at 17.  Indeed, one of the main drafters of the Act explained that the definition of the term "process" in §100(b) reflects "how the courts have construed the term 'art.'&nbsp;"  Tr. of address by Judge Giles S. Rich to the New York Patent Law Association 7-8 (Nov. 6, 1952).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;As discussed above, by this time, courts had consistently construed the term "art" to exclude methods of doing business.  The 1952 Act likely captured that same meaning.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 38" name="FRconcurrence1.38" href="#FNconcurrence1.38">38</a></sup>  Cf. <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 16-17 (reasoning that because a provision of the 1952 Act "paraphrases language which has often been used in decisions of the courts" and was "added to the statute for uniformity and definiteness,&nbsp;" that provision should be treated as "a codification of judicial precedents").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 39" name="FRconcurrence1.39" href="#FNconcurrence1.39">39</a></sup>  Indeed, Judge Rich, the main drafter of the 1952 Act, later explained that "the invention of a more effective organization of the materials in, and the techniques of teaching a course in physics, chemistry, or Russian is not a patentable invention because it is outside of the enumerated categories of 'process, machine, manufacture, or composition of matter, or any new and useful improvement thereof.'&nbsp;"  Principles of Patentability, 28 Geo. Wash. L.&nbsp;Rev. 393, 394 (1960).  "Also outside that group," he added, was a process for doing business: "the greatest inventio[n] of our times, the diaper service."  <em>Ibid</em>.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 40" name="FRconcurrence1.40" href="#FNconcurrence1.40">40</a></sup></p>
<p>

<em>"Anything Under the Sun"</em></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Despite strong evidence that Congress has consistently authorized patents for a limited class of subject matter and that the 1952 Act did not alter the nature of the then-existing limits, petitioners and their <em>amici</em> emphasize a single phrase in the Act's legislative history, which suggests that the statutory subject matter "&nbsp;'include[s] anything under the sun that is made by man.'&nbsp;"  Brief for Petitioners 19 (quoting <em>Chakrabarty</em>, 447 U.&nbsp;S., at 309, in turn quoting S.&nbsp;Rep. 1979, at 5).  Similarly, the Court relies on language from our opinion in <em>Chakrabarty </em>that was based in part on this piece of legislative history.  See <em>ante</em>, at 4, 6.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;This reliance is misplaced.  We have never understood that piece of legislative history to mean that any series of steps is a patentable process.  Indeed, if that were so, then our many opinions analyzing what is a patentable process were simply wastes of pages in the U.&nbsp;S. Reports.  And to accept that errant piece of legislative history as widening the scope of the patent law would contradict other evidence in the congressional record, as well as our presumption that the 1952 Act merely codified the meaning of "process" and did not expand it, see <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 184.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Taken in context, it is apparent that the quoted language has a far less expansive meaning.  The full sentence in the Committee Reports reads: "A person may have 'invented' a machine or a manufacture, which may include anything under the sun that is made by man, but it is not necessarily patentable under section 101 unless the conditions of [this] title are fulfilled."  S. Rep. 1979, at 5; H.&nbsp;R. Rep. 1923, at 6.  Viewed as a whole, it seems clear that this language does not purport to explain that "anything under the sun" is patentable.  Indeed, the language may be understood to state the exact opposite: that "[a] person may have 'invented' ... anything under the sun," but that thing "is not necessarily patentable under section 101."  Thus, even in the <em>Chakrabarty</em> opinion, which relied on this quote, we cautioned that the 1952 Reports did not "suggest that §101 has no limits or that it embraces every discovery." 447 U.&nbsp;S., at 309.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Moreover, even if the language in the Committee Reports was meant to flesh out the meaning of any portion of §101, it did not purport to define the term "process."  The language refers only to "manufacture[s]" and "machine[s]," tangible objects "made by man."  It does not reference the "process" category of subject matter (nor could a process be comfortably described as something "<em>made</em> by man").  The language may also be understood merely as defining the term "invents" in §101.  As Judge Dyk explained in his opinion below, the phrase "made by man" "is reminiscent" of a 1790's description of the limits of English patent law, that an "invention must be 'made by man'&nbsp;" and cannot be "&nbsp;'a philosophical principle only, neither organized or capable of being organized' from a patentable manufacture."  545 F.&nbsp;3d, at 976 (quoting <em>Hornblower</em> v. <em>Boulton,</em> 8 T. R. 95, 98 (K. B. 1799)).  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The 1952 Act, in short, cannot be understood as expanding the scope of patentable subject matter by suggesting that any series of steps may be patented as a "process" under §101.  If anything, the Act appears to have codified the conclusion that subject matter which was understood not to be patentable in 1952 was to remain unpatentable.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Our recent case law reinforces my view that a series of steps for conducting business is not a "process" under §101.  Since Congress passed the 1952 Act, we have never ruled on whether that Act authorizes patents on business methods.  But we have cast significant doubt on that proposition by giving substantial weight to the machine-or-transformation test, as general methods of doing business do not pass that test.  And more recently, Members of this Court have noted that patents on business methods are of "suspect validity."  <em>eBay Inc.</em> v. <em>MercExchange, L. L. C.</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S. 388, 397 (2006) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy, J.</small></span>, concurring).</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*  *  *</p>
</h2><p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Since at least the days of Assyrian merchants, people have devised better and better ways to conduct business.  Yet it appears that neither the Patent Clause, nor early patent law, nor the current §101 contemplated or was publicly understood to mean that such innovations are patentable.  Although it may be difficult to define with precision what is a patentable "process" under §101, the historical clues converge on one conclusion: A business method is not a "process."  And to the extent that there is ambiguity, we should be mindful of our judicial role.  "[W]e must proceed cautiously when we are asked to extend patent rights" into an area that the Patent Act likely was not "enacted to protect," <em>Flook</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S., at 596, 593, lest we create a legal regime that Congress never would have endorsed, and that can be repaired only by disturbing settled property rights.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">V</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Despite the strong historical evidence that a method of doing business does not constitute a "process" under §101, petitioners nonetheless argue--and the Court suggests in dicta, <em>ante</em>, at 10-11--that a subsequent law, the First Inventor Defense Act of 1999, "must be read together" with §101 to make business methods patentable.  Brief for Petitioners 29.  This argument utilizes a flawed method of statutory interpretation and ignores the motivation for the 1999 Act.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In 1999, following a Federal Circuit decision that intimated business methods could be patented, see <em>State Street</em>,<em> </em>149 F.&nbsp;3d 1368, Congress moved quickly to limit the potential fallout.  Congress passed the 1999 Act, codified at 35 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §273, which provides a limited defense to claims of patent infringement, see §273(b), regarding certain "method[s] of doing or conducting business," §273(a)(3).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;It is apparent, both from the content and history of the Act, that Congress did not in any way ratify <em>State Street </em>(or, as petitioners contend, the broadest possible reading of <em>State Street</em>).  The Act merely limited one potential effect of that decision: that businesses might suddenly find themselves liable for innocently using methods they assumed could not be patented.  The Act did not purport to amend the limitations in §101 on eligible subject matter.  Indeed, Congress placed the statute in Part III of Title 35, which addresses "Patents and Protection of Patent Rights," rather than in Part II, which contains §101 and addresses "Patentability of Inventions and Grant of Patents."  Particularly because petitioners' reading of the 1999 Act would expand §101 to cover a category of processes that have not "historically been eligible" for patents, <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 184, we should be loathe to conclude that Congress effectively amended §101 without saying so clearly.  We generally presume that Congress "does not, one might say, hide elephants in mouseholes."  <em>Whitman</em> v. <em>American Trucking Assns., Inc.</em>, 531 U.&nbsp;S. 457, 468 (2001).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Act therefore is, at best, merely evidence of 1999 legislative views on the meaning of the earlier, 1952 Act.  "[T]he views of a subsequent Congress," however, "form a hazardous basis for inferring the intent of an earlier one."  <em>United States</em> v. <em>Price</em>, 361 U.&nbsp;S. 304, 313 (1960).  When a later statute is offered as "an expression of how the ... Congress interpreted a statute passed by another Congress ... a half century before," "such interpretation has very little, if any, significance."  <em>Rainwater</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 356 U.&nbsp;S. 590, 593 (1958).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Furthermore, even assuming that Congress' views at the turn of the 21st century could potentially serve as a valid basis for interpreting a statute passed in the mid-20th century, the First Inventor Defense Act does not aid petitioners because it does not show that the later Congress itself understood §101 to cover business methods.  If anything, it shows that a few judges on the Federal Circuit understood §101 in that manner and that Congress understood what those judges had done.  The Act appears to reflect surprise and perhaps even dismay that business methods might be patented.  Thus, in the months following <em>State Street</em>, congressional authorities lamented that "business methods and processes ... until recently were thought not to be patentable," H.&nbsp;R. Rep. No. 106-464, p.&nbsp;121 (1999); accord, H.&nbsp;R. Rep. No. 106-287, pt. 1, p. 31 (1999).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 41" name="FRconcurrence1.41" href="#FNconcurrence1.41">41</a></sup>  The fact that Congress decided it was appropriate to create a new <em>defense </em>to claims that business method patents were being infringed merely demonstrates recognition that such claims could create a significant new problem for the business community.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court nonetheless states that the 1999 Act "acknowledges that there may be business method patents," thereby "clarify[ing]" its "understanding" of §101.  <em>Ante</em>, at 11.  More specifically, the Court worries that if we were to interpret the 1952 Act to exclude business methods, our interpretation "would render §273 meaningless."  <em>Ibid.  </em>I agree that "[a] statute should be construed so that effect is given to all its provisions."  <em>Corley</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 556 U.&nbsp;S. ___, ___ (2009) (slip op., at 9) (internal quotation marks omitted).  But it is a different matter altogether when the Court construes one statute, the 1952 Act, to give effect to a different statute, the 1999 Act.  The canon on which the Court relies is predicated upon the idea that "[a] statute is passed as a whole."  2A N. Singer &amp; J. Singer, Statutes and Statutory Construction §46:5, p. 189 (7th ed. 2007).  But the two statutes in question were not passed as a whole.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Put another way, we ordinarily assume, quite sensibly, that Congress would not in one statute include two provisions that are at odds with each other.  But as this case shows, that sensible reasoning can break down when applied to different statutes.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 42" name="FRconcurrence1.42" href="#FNconcurrence1.42">42</a></sup>  The 1999 Act was passed to limit the impact of the Federal Circuit's then-recent statements on the 1952 Act.  Although repudiating that judicial dictum (as we should) might effectively render the 1999 Act a nullity going forward, such a holding would not mean that it was a nullity when Congress enacted it.  Section 273 may have been a technically unnecessary response to confusion about patentable subject matter, but it appeared necessary in 1999 in light of what was being discussed in legal circles at the time.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 43" name="FRconcurrence1.43" href="#FNconcurrence1.43">43</a></sup>  Consider the logical implications of the Court's approach to this question: If, tomorrow, Congress were to conclude that patents on business methods are so <em>important </em>that the special infringement defense in §273 ought to be abolished, and thus repealed that provision, this could paradoxically strengthen<em> </em>the case <em>against</em> such patents because there would no longer be a §273 that "acknowledges ... business method patents,"<em> ante</em>, at 11.  That is not a sound method of statutory interpretation.  </p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In light of its history and purpose, I think it obvious that the 1999 Congress would never have enacted §273 if it had foreseen that this Court would rely on the provision as a basis for concluding that business methods are patentable.  Section 273 is a red herring; we should be focusing our attention on §101 itself.  </p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">VI</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The constitutionally mandated purpose and function of the patent laws bolster the conclusion that methods of doing business are not "processes" under §101.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Constitution allows Congress to issue patents "[t]o promote the Progress of ... useful Arts," Art.&nbsp;I, §8, cl.&nbsp;8.  This clause "is both a grant of power and a limitation."  <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 5.  It "reflects a balance between the need to encourage innovation and the avoidance of monopolies which stifle competition without any concomitant advance in the 'Progress of Science and useful Arts.'&nbsp;" <em>Bonito Boats, </em>489 U.&nbsp;S., at 146.  "This is the standard<em> </em>expressed in the Constitution and it may not be ignored.  And it is in this light that patent validity 'requires reference to [the] standard written into the Constitution.'&nbsp;"  <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 6 (quoting <em>Great Atlantic &amp; Pacific Tea Co.</em> v. <em>Supermarket Equipment Corp.</em>, 340 U.&nbsp;S. 147, 154 (1950) (Douglas, J., concurring) (emphasis deleted)); see also <em>Grant</em> v. <em>Raymond</em>, 6 Pet. 218, 241-242 (1832) (explaining that patent "laws which are passed to give effect to this [constitutional] purpose ought, we think, to be construed in the spirit in which they have been made").<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 44" name="FRconcurrence1.44" href="#FNconcurrence1.44">44</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Thus, although it is for Congress to "implement the stated purpose of the Framers by selecting the policy which in its judgment best effectuates the constitutional aim," <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 6, we interpret ambiguous patent laws as a set of rules that "wee[d] out those inventions which would not be disclosed or devised but for the inducement of a patent," <em>id.</em>, at 11, and that "embod[y]" the "careful balance between the need to promote innovation and the recognition that imitation and refinement through imitation are both necessary to invention itself and the very lifeblood of a competitive economy," <em>Bonito Boats</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S., at 146.  And absent a discernible signal from Congress, we proceed cautiously when dealing with patents that press on the limits of the "&nbsp;'standard written into the constitution,'&nbsp;" <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 6, for at the "fringes of congressional power," "more is required of legislatures than a vague delegation to be filled in later," <em>Barenblatt</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 360 U.&nbsp;S. 109, 139-140 (1959) (Black, J., dissenting); see also <em>Greene</em> v. <em>McElroy</em>, 360 U.&nbsp;S. 474, 507 (1959) ("[D]ecisions of great constitutional import and effect" "requir[e] careful and purposeful consideration by those responsible for enacting and implementing our laws").  We should not casually risk exceeding the constitutional limitation on Congress' behalf.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Court has kept this "constitutional standard" in mind when deciding what is patentable subject matter under §101.  For example, we have held that no one can patent "laws of nature, natural phenomena, and abstract ideas."  <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 185.  These "are the basic tools of scientific and technological work," <em>Benson</em>, 409 U.&nbsp;S., at 67, and therefore, if patented, would stifle the very progress that Congress is authorized to promote, see, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>O'Reilly</em>, 15 How., at 113 (explaining that Morse's patent on electromagnetism for writing would preempt a wide swath of technological developments).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Without any legislative guidance to the contrary, there is a real concern that patents on business methods would press on the limits of the "standard<em> </em>expressed in the Constitution," <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 6, more likely stifling progress than "promot[ing]" it.  U.&nbsp;S. Const., Art.&nbsp;I, §8, cl.&nbsp;8.  I recognize that not all methods of doing business are the same, and that therefore the constitutional "balance," <em>Bonito Boats,</em> 489 U.&nbsp;S., at 146, may vary within this category.  Nevertheless, I think that this balance generally supports the historic understanding of the term "process" as excluding business methods.  And a categorical analysis fits with the purpose, as Thomas Jefferson explained, of ensuring that "&nbsp;'every one might know when his actions were safe and lawful,'&nbsp;" <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 10; see also <em>Festo Corp.</em> v. <em>Shoketsu Kinzoku Kogyo Kabushiki Co.</em>, 535 U.&nbsp;S. 722, 730-731 (2002) ("The monopoly is a property right; and like any property right, its boundaries should be clear.  This clarity is essential to promote progress"); <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 219 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., dissenting) (it is necessary to have "rules that enable a conscientious patent lawyer to determine with a fair degree of accuracy" what is patentable).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;On one side of the balance is whether a patent monopoly is necessary to "motivate the innovation," <em>Pfaff </em>v. <em>Wells Electronics, Inc.,</em> 525 U.&nbsp;S. 55, 63 (1998).  Although there is certainly disagreement about the need for patents, scholars generally agree that when innovation is expensive, risky, and easily copied, inventors are less likely to undertake the guaranteed costs of innovation in order to obtain the mere possibility of an invention that others can copy.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 45" name="FRconcurrence1.45" href="#FNconcurrence1.45">45</a></sup>  Both common sense and recent economic scholarship suggest that these dynamics of cost, risk, and reward vary by the type of thing being patented.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 46" name="FRconcurrence1.46" href="#FNconcurrence1.46">46</a></sup>  And the functional case that patents promote progress generally is stronger for subject matter that has "historically been eligible to receive the protection of our patent laws," <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S., at 184, than for methods of doing business.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Many have expressed serious doubts about whether patents are necessary to encourage business innovation.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 47" name="FRconcurrence1.47" href="#FNconcurrence1.47">47</a></sup>  Despite the fact that we have long assumed business methods could not be patented, it has been remarked that "the chief business of the American people, is business."<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 48" name="FRconcurrence1.48" href="#FNconcurrence1.48">48</a></sup>  Federal Express developed an overnight delivery service and a variety of specific methods (including shipping through a central hub and online package tracking) without a patent.  Although counterfactuals are a dubious form of analysis, I find it hard to believe that many of our entrepreneurs forwent business innovation because they could not claim a patent on their new methods.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;"[C]ompanies have ample incentives to develop business methods even without patent protection, because the competitive marketplace rewards companies that use more efficient business methods."  Burk &amp; Lemley 1618.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 49" name="FRconcurrence1.49" href="#FNconcurrence1.49">49</a></sup>  Innovators often capture advantages from new business methods notwithstanding the risk of others copying their innovation.  Some business methods occur in secret and therefore can be protected with trade secrecy.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 50" name="FRconcurrence1.50" href="#FNconcurrence1.50">50</a></sup>  And for those methods that occur in public, firms that innovate often capture long-term benefits from doing so, thanks to various first mover advantages, including lockins, branding, and networking effects.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 51" name="FRconcurrence1.51" href="#FNconcurrence1.51">51</a></sup>  Business innovation, moreover, generally does not entail the same kinds of risk as does more traditional, technological innovation.  It generally does not require the same "enormous costs in terms of time, research, and development," <em>Bicron</em>, 416 U.&nbsp;S., at 480, and thus does not require the same kind of "compensation to [innovators] for their labor, toil, and expense," <em>Seymour</em> v. <em>Osborne</em>, 11 Wall. 516, 533-544 (1871).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 52" name="FRconcurrence1.52" href="#FNconcurrence1.52">52</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;Nor, in many cases, would patents on business methods promote progress by encouraging "public disclosure."  <em>Pfaff</em>, 525 U. S., at 63; see also <em>Brenner</em> v. <em>Manson</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S. 519, 533 (1966) ("[O]ne of the purposes of the patent system is to encourage dissemination of information concerning discoveries and inventions").  Many business methods are practiced in public, and therefore a patent does not necessarily encourage the dissemination of anything not already known.  And for the methods practiced in private, the benefits of disclosure may be small: Many such methods are distributive, not productive--that is, they do not generate any efficiency but only provide a means for competitors to one-up each other in a battle for pieces of the pie.  And as the Court has explained, "it is hard to see how the public would be benefited by disclosure" of certain business tools, since the nondisclosure of these tools "encourages businesses to initiate new and individualized plans of operation," which "in turn, leads to a greater variety of business methods."  <em>Bicron</em>, 416 U.&nbsp;S., at 483.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In any event, even if patents on business methods were useful for encouraging innovation and disclosure, it would still be questionable whether they would, on balance, facilitate or impede the progress of American business.  For even when patents encourage innovation and disclosure, "<em>too much</em> patent protection can impede rather than 'promote the Progress of ... useful Arts.'&nbsp;" <em>Laboratory Corp. of America Holdings </em>v. <em>Metabolite Laboratories, Inc.</em>,<em> </em>548 U.&nbsp;S. 124, 126-127 (2006) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer,</small></span> J., dissenting from dismissal of certiorari).  Patents "can discourage research by impeding the free exchange of information," for example, by forcing people to "avoid the use of potentially patented ideas, by leading them to conduct costly and time-consuming searches of existing or pending patents, by requiring complex licensing arrangements, and by raising the costs of using the patented" methods.  <em>Id.</em>, at 127.  Although "[e]very patent is the grant of a privilege of exacting tolls from the public," <em>Great Atlantic</em>, 340 U.&nbsp;S., at 154 (Douglas, J., concurring), the tolls of patents on business methods may be especially high.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The primary concern is that patents on business methods may prohibit a wide swath of legitimate competition and innovation.  As one scholar explains, "it is useful to conceptualize knowledge as a pyramid: the big ideas are on top; specific applications are at the bottom." Dreyfuss 275.  The higher up a patent is on the pyramid, the greater the social cost and the greater the hindrance to further innovation.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 53" name="FRconcurrence1.53" href="#FNconcurrence1.53">53</a></sup>  Thus, this Court stated in <em>Benson</em> that "[p]henomena of nature ... , mental processes, and abstract intellectual concepts are not patentable, as they are the basic tools of scientific and technological work," 409 U. S., at 67; see also, <em>Joseph E. Seagram &amp; Sons, Inc.</em>, 180 F.&nbsp;2d, at 28 ("To give appellant a monopoly, through the issuance of a patent, upon so great an area ... would in our view impose without warrant of law a serious restraint upon the advance of science and industry").  Business methods are similarly often closer to "big ideas," as they are the basic tools of <em>commercial </em>work.  They are also, in many cases, the basic tools of further business innovation: Innovation in business methods is often a sequential and complementary process in which imitation may be a "<em>spur</em> to innovation" and patents may "become an <em>impediment</em>."  Bessen &amp; Maskin, Sequential Innovation, Patents, and Imitation, 40 RAND J. Econ. 611, 613 (2009).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 54" name="FRconcurrence1.54" href="#FNconcurrence1.54">54</a></sup>  "Think how the airline industry might now be structured if the first company to offer frequent flyer miles had enjoyed the sole right to award them."  Dreyfuss 264.  "[I]mitation and refinement through imitation are both necessary to invention itself and the very lifeblood of a competitive economy."  <em>Bonito Boats</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S., at 146.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;If business methods could be patented, then many business decisions, no matter how small, could be <em>potential</em> patent violations.  Businesses would either live in constant fear of litigation or would need to undertake the costs of searching through patents that describe methods of doing business, attempting to decide whether their innovation is one that remains in the public domain.  See Long, Information Costs in Patent and Copyright, 90 Va. L.&nbsp;Rev. 465, 487-488 (2004) (hereinafter Long).  But as we have long explained, patents should not "embaras[s] the honest pursuit of business with fears and apprehensions of concealed liens and unknown liabilities to lawsuits and vexatious accountings for profits made in good faith."  <em>Atlantic Works</em> v. <em>Brady</em>, 107 U.&nbsp;S. 192, 200 (1883).<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 55" name="FRconcurrence1.55" href="#FNconcurrence1.55">55</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;These effects are magnified by the "potential vagueness" of business method patents, <em>eBay Inc.</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 397 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, J., concurring).  When it comes to patents, "clarity is essential to promote progress."  <em>Festo Corp.</em>, 535 U.&nbsp;S., at 730-731.  Yet patents on methods of conducting business generally are composed largely or entirely of intangible steps.  Compared to "the kinds of goods ... around which patent rules historically developed," it thus tends to be more costly and time consuming to search through, and to negotiate licenses for, patents on business methods.  See Long 539, 470.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 56" name="FRconcurrence1.56" href="#FNconcurrence1.56">56</a></sup></p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The breadth of business methods, their omnipresence in our society, and their potential vagueness also invite a particularly pernicious use of patents that we have long criticized.  As early as the 19th century, we explained that the patent laws are not intended to "creat[e] a class of speculative schemers who make it their business to watch the advancing wave of improvement, and gather its foam in the form of patented monopolies, which enable them to lay a heavy tax upon the industry of the country, without contributing anything to the real advancement of the arts."  <em>Atlantic Works</em>, 107 U.&nbsp;S., at 200.  Yet business method patents may have begun to do exactly that.  See <em>eBay Inc.</em>, 547 U.&nbsp;S., at 396-397 (opinion of <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Kennedy</small></span>, J.).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;These many costs of business method patents not only may stifle innovation, but they are also likely to "stifle competition," <em>Bonito Boats, </em>489 U.&nbsp;S., at 146.  Even if a business method patent is ultimately held invalid, patent holders may be able to use it to threaten litigation and to bully competitors, especially those that cannot bear the costs of a drawn out, fact-intensive patent litigation.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 57" name="FRconcurrence1.57" href="#FNconcurrence1.57">57</a></sup>  That can take a particular toll on small and upstart businesses.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 58" name="FRconcurrence1.58" href="#FNconcurrence1.58">58</a></sup>  Of course, patents always serve as a barrier to competition for the type of subject matter that is patented.  But patents on business methods are patents on business itself.  Therefore, unlike virtually every other category of patents, they are by their very nature likely to depress the dynamism of the marketplace.<sup><a aria-label="Move to footnote 59" name="FRconcurrence1.59" href="#FNconcurrence1.59">59</a></sup></p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">*  *  *</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The constitutional standard for patentability is difficult to apply with any precision, and Congress has significant discretion to "implement the stated purpose of the Framers by selecting the policy which in its judgment best effectuates the constitutional aim," <em>Graham</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S., at 6.  But Congress has not, either explicitly or implicitly, determined that patents on methods of doing business would effectuate this aim.  And as I understand their practical consequences, it is hard to see how they would.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">VII</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;The Constitution grants to Congress an important power to promote innovation.  In its exercise of that power, Congress has established an intricate system of intellectual property.  The scope of patentable subject matter under that system is broad.  But it is not endless.  In the absence of any clear guidance from Congress, we have only limited textual, historical, and functional clues on which to rely.  Those clues all point toward the same conclusion: that petitioners' claim is not a "process" within the meaning of §101 because methods of doing business are not, in themselves, covered by the statute.  In my view, acknowledging as much would be a far more sensible and restrained way to resolve this case.  Accordingly, while I concur in the judgment, I strongly disagree with the Court's disposition of this case.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<a name="concurrence2"></a>



<center><h2><p align="CENTER">BERNARD L. BILSKI <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>and</small></span> RAND A. WARSAW, PETITIONERS <em>v.</em> DAVID J. KAPPOS, UNDER SECRETARY OF COMMERCE FOR INTELLECTUAL PROPERTY AND DIRECTOR, PATENT AND TRADEMARK OFFICE</p>
</h2></center><center><h4><p align="CENTER">on writ of certiorari to the united states court of appeals for the federal circuit</p>
</h4></center><center><p align="CENTER">[June 28, 2010]</p>
</center><p></p><hr>

<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Breyer</small></span>, with whom <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> joins as to Part II, concurring in the judgment.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">I</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I agree with <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens</small></span> that a "general method of engaging in business transactions" is not a patentable "process" within the meaning of 35 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §101.  <em>Ante, </em>at 2 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment).  This Court has never before held that so-called "business methods" are patentable, and, in my view, the text, history, and purposes of the Patent Act make clear that they are not.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10-47.  I would therefore decide this case on that ground, and I join <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens</small></span>' opinion in full.</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;I write separately, however, in order to highlight the substantial <em>agreement</em> among many Members<em> </em>of the Court on many of the fundamental issues of patent law raised by this case.  In light of the need for clarity and settled law in this highly technical area, I think it appropriate to do so.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">II</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In addition to the Court's unanimous agreement that the claims at issue here are unpatentable abstract ideas, it is my view that the following four points are consistent with both the opinion of the Court and <span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Stevens</small></span>' opinion concurring in the judgment:</p>
<p>&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>First, </em>although the text of §101 is broad, it is not without limit.  See <em>ante</em>, at 4-5 (opinion of the Court); <em>ante, </em>at 10 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment).  "[T]he underlying policy of the patent system [is] that 'the things which are worth to the public the embarrassment of an exclusive patent,' . . . must outweigh the restrictive effect of the limited patent monopoly."  <em>Graham</em> v. <em>John Deere Co. of Kansas City</em>, 383 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 10-11 (1966) (quoting Letter from Thomas Jefferson to Isaac McPherson (Aug. 13, 1813), in 6 Writings of Thomas Jefferson 181 (H. Washington ed.)).  The Court has thus been careful in interpreting the Patent Act to "determine not only what is protected, but also what is free for all to use."  <em>Bonito Boats, Inc.</em> v. <em>Thunder Craft Boats, Inc.</em>, 489 U.&nbsp;S. 141, 151 (1989).  In particular, the Court has long held that "[p]henomena of nature, though just discovered, mental processes, and abstract intellectual concepts are not patentable" under §101, since allowing individuals to patent these fundamental principles would "wholly pre-empt" the public's access to the "basic tools of scientific and technological work."  <em>Gottschalk</em> v. <em>Benson</em>, 409 U.&nbsp;S. 63, 67, 72 (1972); see also, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Diamond</em> v. <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S. 175, 185 (1981); <em>Diamond</em> v. <em>Chakrabarty</em>, 447 U.&nbsp;S. 303, 309 (1980).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Second</em>, in a series of cases that extend back over a century, the Court has stated that "[t]ransformation and reduction of an article to a different state or thing is <em>the clue</em> to the patentability of a process claim that does not include particular machines."  <em>Diehr</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 184 (emphasis added; internal quotation marks omitted); see also, <em>e.g.,</em> <em>Benson</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 70; <em>Parker</em> v. <em>Flook</em>, 437 U.&nbsp;S. 584, 588, n.&nbsp;9 (1978); <em>Cochrane</em> v. <em>Deener</em>, 94 U.&nbsp;S. 780, 788 (1877).  Application of this test, the so-called "machine-or-transformation test," has thus repeatedly helped the Court to determine what is "a patentable 'process.'&nbsp;" <em>Flook</em>, <em>supra, </em>at 589.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Third</em>, while the machine-or-transformation test has always been a "useful and important clue," it has never been the "sole test" for determining patentability.  <em>Ante, </em>at 8; see also <em>ante, </em>at 1 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment); <em>Benson</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 71 (rejecting the argument that "no process patent could ever qualify" for protection under §101 "if it did not meet the [machine-or-transformation] requirements").  Rather, the Court has emphasized that a process claim meets the requirements of §101 when, "considered as a whole," it "is performing a function which the patent laws were designed to protect (<em>e.g.</em>, transforming or reducing an article to a different state or thing)."  <em>Diehr</em>, <em>supra, </em>at 192.  The machine-or-transformation test is thus an <em>important</em> <em>example</em> of how a court can determine patentability under §101, but the Federal Circuit erred in this case by treating it as the <em>exclusive test</em>.</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;<em>Fourth</em>, although the machine-or-transformation test is not the only test for patentability, this by no means indicates that anything which produces a "&nbsp;'useful, concrete, and tangible result,'&nbsp;" <em>State Street Bank &amp; Trust Co. </em>v. <em>Signature Financial Group, Inc., </em>149 F.&nbsp;3d 1368, 1373 (CA Fed. 1998), is patentable.  "[T]his Court has never made such a statement and, if taken literally, the statement would cover instances where this Court has held the contrary."  <em>Laboratory Corp. of America Holdings</em> v. <em>Metabolite Laboratories</em>, <em>Inc.</em>, 548 U.&nbsp;S. 124, 136 (2006) (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Breyer</small></span>, J., dissenting from dismissal of certiorari as improvidently granted); see also, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>O'Reilly</em> v. <em>Morse</em>, 15 How. 62, 117 (1854); <em>Flook</em>, <em>supra</em>, at 590.  Indeed, the introduction of the "useful, concrete, and tangible result" approach to patentability, associated with the Federal Circuit's <em>State Street</em> decision, preceded the granting of patents that "ranged from the somewhat ridiculous to the truly absurd."  <em>In&nbsp;re Bilski</em>, 545 F.&nbsp;3d 943, 1004 (CA Fed. 2008) (Mayer, J., dissenting) (citing patents on, <em>inter alia</em>, a "method of training janitors to dust and vacuum using video displays," a "system for toilet reservations," and a "method of using color-coded bracelets to designate dating status in order to limit 'the embarrassment of rejection'&nbsp;"); see also Brief for Respondent 40-41, and n.&nbsp;20 (listing dubious patents).  To the extent that the Federal Circuit's decision in this case rejected that approach, nothing in today's decision should be taken as disapproving of that determination.  See <em>ante</em>, at 16; <em>ante</em>, at 2, n.&nbsp;1 (<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Stevens</small></span>, J., concurring in judgment).</p>
<p>

&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;In sum, it is my view that, in reemphasizing that the "machine-or-transformation" test is not necessarily the <em>sole </em>test of patentability, the Court intends neither to de-emphasize the test's usefulness nor to suggest that many patentable processes lie beyond its reach.</p>
<h2><p align="CENTER">III</p>
</h2><p>
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;With these observations, I concur in the Court's judgment.</p>
<p></p><hr>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" href="#FRopinion1.*">*</a></strong><br><p>*&nbsp;<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small>Justice Scalia</small></span> does not join Parts II-B-2 and II-C-2.</p>

<br><h3>FOOTNOTES</h3><br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 1" name="FNconcurrence1.1" href="#FRconcurrence1.1">1</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Even if the machine-or-transformation test may not define the scope of a patentable process, it would be a grave mistake to assume that anything with a "&nbsp;'useful, concrete and tangible result,'&nbsp;" <em>State Street Bank &amp; Trust</em> v.<em> Signature Financial Group, Inc.,</em> 149 F.&nbsp;3d 1368, 1373 (CA Fed. 1998), may be patented.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 2" name="FNconcurrence1.2" href="#FRconcurrence1.2">2</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For example, a rule that broadly-phrased claims cannot constitute patentable processes could call into question our approval of Alexander Graham Bell's famous fifth claim on "&nbsp;'[t]he method of, and apparatus for, transmitting vocal or other sounds telegraphically, as herein described, by causing electrical undulations, similar in form to the vibrations of the air accompanying the said vocal or other sounds, substantially as set forth,'&nbsp;" <em>The Telephone Cases</em>, 126 U.&nbsp;S. 1, 531 (1888).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 3" name="FNconcurrence1.3" href="#FRconcurrence1.3">3</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court quotes our decision in <em>Diamond</em> v. <em>Chakrabarty</em>, 447 U.&nbsp;S. 303 (1980), for the proposition that, "&nbsp;'[i]n choosing such expansive terms ... modified by the comprehensive "any," Congress plainly contemplated that the patent laws would be given wide scope.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 4.  But<span style="text-transform: uppercase;"><small> </small></span>the Court fails to mention which terms we were discussing in <em>Chakrabarty:</em> the terms "manufacture" and "composition of matter."  See 447 U.&nbsp;S., at 308 ("In choosing such expansive terms as 'manufacture' and 'composition of matter,' modified by the comprehensive 'any,' Congress plainly contemplated that the patent laws would be given wide scope").  As discussed herein, Congress' choice of the term "process" reflected a background understanding of what sorts of series of steps could be patented, and likely reflected an intentional design to codify that settled, judicial understanding.  This may not have been the case with the terms at issue in <em>Chakrabarty.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 4" name="FNconcurrence1.4" href="#FRconcurrence1.4">4</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For example, if this Court were to interpret the Sherman Act according to the Act's plain text, it could prohibit "the entire body of private contract," <em>National Soc. of Professional Engineers</em> v. <em>United States</em>, 435 U.&nbsp;S. 679, 688 (1978).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 5" name="FNconcurrence1.5" href="#FRconcurrence1.5">5</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court attempts to avoid such absurd results by stating that these "[c]oncerns" "can be met by making sure that the claim meets the requirements of §101."  <em>Ante</em>, at 6.  Because the only limitation on the plain meaning of "process" that the Court acknowledges explicitly is the bar on abstract ideas, laws of nature, and the like, it is presumably this limitation that is left to stand between all conceivable human activity and patent monopolies.  But many processes that would make for absurd patents are not abstract ideas.  Nor can the requirements of novelty, nonobviousness, and particular description pick up the slack.  Cf. <em>ante</em>, at 12-13 (plurality opinion).  A great deal of human activity was at some time novel and nonobvious.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 6" name="FNconcurrence1.6" href="#FRconcurrence1.6">6</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Curiously, the Court concedes that "these exceptions are not required by the statutory text," but urges that "they are <em>consistent</em> with the notion that a patentable process must be 'new and useful.'&nbsp;"  <em>Ante</em>, at 5 (emphasis added).  I do not see how these exceptions find a textual home in the term "new and useful."  The exceptions may be consistent with those words, but they are sometimes inconsistent with the "ordinary, contemporary, common meaning," <em>ante,</em> at 6, 10 (internal quotation marks omitted), of the words "process" and "method."</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 7" name="FNconcurrence1.7" href="#FRconcurrence1.7">7</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Pennock</em> v. <em>Dialogue</em>, 2 Pet. 1, 18 (1829) ("[M]any of the provisions of our patent act are derived from the principles and practice, which have prevailed in the construction of that of England"); Proceedings in Congress During the Years 1789 and 1790 Relating to the First Patent and Copyright Laws, 22 J. Pat. Off. Soc. 352, 363 (1940) (explaining that the 1790 Patent Act was "framed according to the Course of Practice in the English Patent Office"); see also Walterscheid, The Early Evolution of the United States Patent Law: Antecedents, 76 J. Pat. &amp; Trademark Off. Soc. 697, 698 (1994) (describing the role of the English backdrop).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 8" name="FNconcurrence1.8" href="#FRconcurrence1.8">8</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Hornblower</em> v. <em>Boulton, </em>8 T. R. 95 <em> </em>(K. B. 1799).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 9" name="FNconcurrence1.9" href="#FRconcurrence1.9">9</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Roebuck</em> <em>and Garbett</em> v. <em>William Stirling &amp; Son</em> (H. L. 1774), reprinted in 1 T. Webster, Reports and Notes of Cases on Letters Patent for Inventions 45 (1844) ("method of making acid spirit by burning sulphur and saltpetre, and collecting the condensed fumes"); <em>id.</em>, at 77 ("&nbsp;'method of producing a yellow colour for painting in oil or water, making white lead, and separating the mineral alkali from common salt, all to be performed in one single process'&nbsp;"); see also C. MacLeod, Inventing the Industrial Revolution: The English Patent System, 1660-1800, pp. 84-93, 100-104, 109-110, 152-155 (1988) (listing patents) (hereinafter MacLeod).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 10" name="FNconcurrence1.10" href="#FRconcurrence1.10">10</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some English cases made reference to the permissibility of patents over new "trades."  But so far as I can tell, the term "trade" referred not to the methods of conducting business but rather to methods of making and using physical items or to the object of the trade.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Clothworkers of Ipswich Case</em>,<em> </em>78 Eng. Rep. 147, 148 (K. B. 1603) ("[I]f a man hath brought in a new invention and a new trade within the kingdom ... [the King] may grant by charter unto him").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 12" name="FNconcurrence1.12" href="#FRconcurrence1.12">12</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>id.,</em> at 95; B. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 14" name="FNconcurrence1.14" href="#FRconcurrence1.14">14</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also Renn, John Knox's Plan for Insuring Lives: A Patent of Invention in 1778, 101 J. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 15" name="FNconcurrence1.15" href="#FRconcurrence1.15">15</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;"The English patent system" at that time "was one of simple registration.  Extensive scrutiny was not expected of the law officers administering it."  MacLeod 41.  Thus, as one scholar suggested of the patent on life insurance, "perhaps the Law Officer was in a very good humour that day, or perhaps he had forgotten the wording of the statute; most likely he was concerned only with the promised 'very considerable Consumption of [Revenue] Stamps' which [the patent holder] declared, would 'contribute to the increase of the Public Revenues.'&nbsp;"  Renn 285.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 16" name="FNconcurrence1.16" href="#FRconcurrence1.16">16</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See <em>Markman</em> v. <em>Westview Instruments, Inc.</em>, 517 U.&nbsp;S. 370, 381 (1996) ("[T]he state of patent law in the common-law courts before 1800 led one historian to observe that 'the reported cases are destitute of any decision of importance'&nbsp;" (quoting Hulme, On the Consideration of the Patent Grant, Past and Present, 13 L.&nbsp;Q. Rev. 313, 318 (1897))); MacLeod 1, 61-62 (explaining the dearth of clear case law); see also <em>Boulton</em> v. <em>Bull,</em> 2 H. Bl. 463, 491, 126 Eng. Rep. 651, 665 (C. P. 1795) (Eyre, C. J.) ("Patent rights are no where that I can find accurately discussed in our books").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 17" name="FNconcurrence1.17" href="#FRconcurrence1.17">17</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, A. Hist. 610, 624-625 (1994).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 18" name="FNconcurrence1.18" href="#FRconcurrence1.18">18</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Pollack 97-100.  For example, those who held patents on oil lamps developed firms that contracted to provide street lighting.  See M. <br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 21" name="FNconcurrence1.21" href="#FRconcurrence1.21">21</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Nor, so far as I can tell, were business method patents common in the United States in the brief period between independence and the creation of our Constitution--despite the fact that it was a time of great business innovation, including new processes for engaging in risky trade and transport, one of which has been called "the quintessential business innovation of the 1780s."  T. Doerflinger, A Vigorous Spirit of Enterprise: Merchants and Economic Development in Revolutionary Philadelphia 291 (1986) (describing new methods of conducting and financing trade with China).</p>
