<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 26" name="FNconcurrence1.26" href="#FRconcurrence1.26">26</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Some scholars suggest that Webster's "close proximity to the Constitutional Convention coupled with his familiarity with the delegates makes it likely that he played some indirect role in the development" of the Constitution's Intellectual Property Clause--a Clause that established not only the power to create patents but also copyrights, a subject in which Webster had great interest.  Donner, Copyright Clause of the U.&nbsp;S. Constitution: Why Did the Framers Include It With Unanimous Approval?  36 Am. J. Legal. Hist. 361, 372 (1992).  But there is no direct evidence of this fact.  See Walterscheid, Background and Origin 40-41.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 27" name="FNconcurrence1.27" href="#FRconcurrence1.27">27</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, 1 S. Johnson, Dictionary of the English Language (1773) (reprint 1978) (listing as definitions of an "art":"[t]he power of doing something not taught by nature and instinct," "[a] science; as, the liberal <em>arts</em>," "[a] trade," "[a]rtfulness; skill; dexterity," "[c]unning," and "[s]peculation").  One might question the breadth of these definitions.  This same dictionary offered as an example of "doing something not taught by nature and instinct," the art<em> </em>of "dance"; and as an example of a "trade," the art of "making sugar."  <em>Ibid.</em></p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 28" name="FNconcurrence1.28" href="#FRconcurrence1.28">28</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For examples of this usage, see Book of Trades or Library of Useful Arts (1807) (describing in a three-volume work 68 trades, each of which is the means of creating a product, such as feather worker or cork cutter); 1 J. Bigelow, The Useful Arts Considered in Connexion with the Applications of Science (1840) (surveying a history of what we would today call mechanics, technology, and engineering).  See also D. Defoe, A General History of Discoveries and Improvements, in Useful Arts (1727); T. Coxe, An Address to an Assembly of the Friends of American Manufactures 17-18 (1787); G. Logan, A Letter to the Citizens of Pennsylvania, on the Necessity of Promoting Agriculture, Manufactures, and the Useful Arts 12-13 (2d ed. 1800); W. Kenrick, An Address to the Artists and Manufacturers of Great Britain 21-38 (1774); cf. <em>Corning</em> v. <em>Burden</em>, 15 How. 252, 267 (1854) (listing the "arts of tanning, dyeing, making water-proof cloth, vulcanizing India rubber, [and] smelting ores").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 29" name="FNconcurrence1.29" href="#FRconcurrence1.29">29</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, 1 D. Chisum, Patents G1-23 (2010); Lutz, Patents and Science: A Clarification of the Patent Clause of the U.&nbsp;S. Constitution, 18 Geo. Wash. L.&nbsp;Rev. 50, 54 (1949-1950); Samuelson, <em>Benson</em> Revisited: The Case Against Patent Protection for Algorithms and Other Computer-Related Inventions, 39 Emory L.&nbsp;J. 1025, 1033, n. 24 (1990); Seidel 10, 13; see also <em>Great Atlantic &amp; Pacific Tea Co.</em> v. <em>Supermarket Equipment Corp.</em>, 340 U.&nbsp;S. 147, 154 (1950) (Douglas, J., concurring) (explaining that in the Framers' view, an "invention, to justify a patent, had to serve the ends of science--to push back the frontiers of chemistry, physics, and the like; to make a distinctive contribution to scientific knowledge"); <em>In&nbsp;re Waldbaum</em>, 457 F.&nbsp;2d 997, 1003 (CCPA 1972) (Rich, J., concurring) ("&nbsp;'The phrase "technological arts," as we have used it, is synonymous with the phrase "useful arts" as it appears in Article I, Section 8 of the Constitution'&nbsp;"); <em>Paulik</em> v. <em>Rizkalla</em>, 760 F.&nbsp;2d 1270, 1276 (CA Fed. 1985) (explaining that "useful arts" is "the process today called technological innovation"); Thomas, The Post-Industrial Patent System, 10 Fordham Intell. Prop. Media &amp; Ent. L.&nbsp;J. 3, 32-55 (1999) (cataloguing early understandings of technological arts).  This view may be supported, for example, by an 1814 grant to Harvard University to create a "Professorship on the Application of Science to the Useful Arts," something that today might be akin to applied science or engineering. See M. James, Engineering an Environment for Change: Bigelow, Peirce, and Early Nineteenth-Century Practical Education at Harvard, in Science at Harvard University: Historical Perspectives 59 (C. Elliott &amp; M. Rossiter eds. 1992).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 30" name="FNconcurrence1.30" href="#FRconcurrence1.30">30</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Walterscheid, To Promote the Progress 173-178; Pollack 107-108.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 31" name="FNconcurrence1.31" href="#FRconcurrence1.31">31</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;These doubts ended by the time of <em>Cochrane</em> v. <em>Deener</em>, 94 U.&nbsp;S. 780 (1877), in which we held that "a process may be patentable irrespective of the particular form of the instrumentalities used," and therefore one may patent "an act, or series of acts, performed upon the subject matter to be transformed and reduced to a different state or thing."  <em>Id.</em>, at 788.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 32" name="FNconcurrence1.32" href="#FRconcurrence1.32">32</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;A skeptic of patents, Jefferson described this as "drawing a line between things which are worth to the public the embarrassment of a patent, and those which are not."  13 Writings of Thomas Jefferson 335 (Memorial ed. 1904).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 33" name="FNconcurrence1.33" href="#FRconcurrence1.33">33</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, <em>Expanded Metal Co.</em> v. <em>Bradford</em>, 214 U.&nbsp;S. 366, 383, 385-386 (1909); <em>The Telephone Cases</em>, 126 U.&nbsp;S., at 533-537; <em>Cochrane</em>, 94 U.&nbsp;S., at 787-788; <em>Burden</em>, 15 How., at 267-268.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 34" name="FNconcurrence1.34" href="#FRconcurrence1.34">34</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also 1 A. Deller, Walker on Patents §26, p. 152 (2d ed. 1964) (A "&nbsp;'system' or method of transacting business is not [a process], nor does it come within any other designation of patentable subject matter").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 35" name="FNconcurrence1.35" href="#FRconcurrence1.35">35</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Although a few patents issued before 1952 that related to methods of doing business, see United States Patent and Trademark Office, Automated Financial or Management Data Processing Methods, online at http://www.uspto.gov/web/menu/busmethp/index.html (all Internet materials as visited June 26, 2010, and available in Clerk of Court's case file), these patents were rare, often issued through self-registration rather than any formalized patent examination, generally were not upheld by courts, and arguably are distinguishable from pure patents on business methods insofar as they often involved the manufacture of new objects.  See <em>In&nbsp;re Bilski</em>, 545 F.&nbsp;3d 943, 974, and n.&nbsp;18 (CA Fed. 2008) (case below) (Dyk, J., concurring); Pollack 74-75; Walterscheid, To Promote the Progress 243.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 36" name="FNconcurrence1.36" href="#FRconcurrence1.36">36</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;For examples of such usage, see <em>The Telephone Cases</em>, 126 U.&nbsp;S., at 533, and <em>Burden</em>, 15 How., at 267.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 37" name="FNconcurrence1.37" href="#FRconcurrence1.37">37</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also 98 Cong. Rec. A415 (1952) (remarks of Rep. Bryson) (describing, after the fact, the 1952 Patent Act, and explaining that "[t]he word 'art' was changed to 'process' in order to clarify its meaning.  No change in substance was intended").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 38" name="FNconcurrence1.38" href="#FRconcurrence1.38">38</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The 1952 Act also retained the language "invents or discovers," which by that time had taken on a connotation that would tend to exclude business methods.  See B. Evans &amp; C. Evans, A Dictionary of Contemporary Usage 137 (1957) (explaining that "discover; invent" means "to make or create something new, especially, in modern usage, something ingeniously devised to perform mechanical operations").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 39" name="FNconcurrence1.39" href="#FRconcurrence1.39">39</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;As explained in Part II, <em>supra</em>, the Court engages in a Jekyll-and-Hyde form of interpretation with respect to the word "process" in §101.  It rejects the interpretation I proffer because the words "process" and "method" do not, on their face, distinguish between different series of acts.  <em>Ante</em>, at 10.  But it also rejects many sorts of processes without a textual basis for doing so.  See <em>ante</em>, at 4-5, 7, 12-15.  And while the Courts rests a great deal of weight on <em>Parker </em>v. <em>Flook,</em> 437 U.&nbsp;S. 584 (1978), for its analysis of abstract ideas, the Court minimizes <em>Flook</em>'s rejection of "a purely literal reading of §101," as well as <em>Flook</em>'s reliance on the historical backdrop of §101 and our understanding of what "the statute was enacted to protect," <em>id.,</em> at 588-590, 593; see also <em>Diamond</em> v. <em>Diehr</em>, 450 U.&nbsp;S. 175, 192 (1981) (explaining that a "claim satisfies the requirements of §101" when it "is performing a function which the patent laws were designed to protect").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 40" name="FNconcurrence1.40" href="#FRconcurrence1.40">40</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Forty years later, Judge Rich authored the <em>State Street </em>opinion that some have understood to make business methods patentable.  But <em>State Street </em>dealt with whether a piece of software could be patented and addressed only claims directed at machines, not processes.  His opinion may therefore be better understood merely as holding that an otherwise patentable process is not unpatentable simply because it is directed toward the conduct of doing business--an issue the Court has no occasion to address today.  See <em>State Street,</em> 149 F.&nbsp;3d, at 1375.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 41" name="FNconcurrence1.41" href="#FRconcurrence1.41">41</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also 145 Cong. Rec. 30985 (1999) (remarks of Sen. Schumer) (explaining that "[i]n <em>State Street,</em> the Court did away with the so-called 'business methods' exception to statutory patentable subject matter," and "[t]he first inventor defense will provide ... important, needed protections in the face of the uncertainty presented by ... the <em>State Street</em> case"); <em>id.,</em> at 31007 (remarks of Sen. DeWine) ("Virtually no one in the industry believed that these methods or processes were patentable"); <em>id.,</em> at 19281 (remarks of Rep. Manzullo) ("Before the State Street Bank and Trust case ... it was universally thought that methods of doing or conducting business were not patentable items").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 42" name="FNconcurrence1.42" href="#FRconcurrence1.42">42</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;The Court opines that "[t]his principle, <em>of course</em>, applies to interpreting any two provisions in the U.&nbsp;S. Code, even when Congress enacted the provisions at different times."  <em>Ante</em>, at 11 (emphasis added).  The only support the Court offers for this proposition is a 1937 opinion for three Justices, in <em>Hague</em> v. <em>Committee for Industrial Organization</em>, 307 U.&nbsp;S. 496, 528-530 (1939) (opinion of Stone, J.).  But that opinion is inapposite.  Although Justice Stone stated that two provisions "must be read together," <em>id.</em>, at 530, he did so to explain that an ambiguity in a later-in-time statute must be understood in light of the earlier-in-time framework against which the ambiguous statute was passed, <em>id.</em>, at 528-530, particularly because the later statute explicitly stated that it "shall not be construed to apply" to the provision created by an earlier Act, <em>id.</em>, at 528.    </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 43" name="FNconcurrence1.43" href="#FRconcurrence1.43">43</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;I am not trying to "overcome" an "established rule of statutory interpretation" with "judicial speculation as to the subjective intent of various legislators," <em>ante</em>, at 11, but, rather, I am explaining why the Court has illogically expanded the canon upon which it relies beyond that canon's logical underpinnings.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 44" name="FNconcurrence1.44" href="#FRconcurrence1.44">44</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also <em>Quanta Computer, Inc.</em> v. <em>LG Electronics, Inc.</em>, 553 U.&nbsp;S. 617, 626 (2008) ("&nbsp;'[T]he primary purpose of our patent laws is not the creation of private fortunes for the owners of patents but is "to promote the progress of science and useful arts"&nbsp;'&nbsp;" (quoting <em>Motion Picture Patents Co.</em> v. <em>Universal Film Mfg. Co.</em>, 243 U.&nbsp;S. 502, 511 (1917))); <em>Pfaff</em> v. <em>Wells Electronics, Inc.</em>, 525 U.&nbsp;S. 55, 63 (1998) ("[T]he patent system represents a carefully crafted bargain that encourages both the creation and the public disclosure of new and useful advances in technology").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 45" name="FNconcurrence1.45" href="#FRconcurrence1.45">45</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See generally W. Landes &amp; R. Posner, The Economic Structure of Intellectual Property Law 13-15 (2003).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 46" name="FNconcurrence1.46" href="#FRconcurrence1.46">46</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, Burk &amp; Lemley, Policy Levers in Patent Law, 89 Va. L.&nbsp;Rev. 1575, 1577-1589 (2003) (hereinafter Burk &amp; Lemley).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 47" name="FNconcurrence1.47" href="#FRconcurrence1.47">47</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See, <em>e.g.</em>, Burk &amp; Lemley 1618; Carrier, Unraveling the Patent-Antitrust Paradox, 150 U.&nbsp;Pa. L.&nbsp;Rev. 761, 826 (2002) (hereinafter Carrier); Dreyfuss, Are Business Methods Patents Bad for Business?  16 Santa Clara Computer &amp; High Tech. L.&nbsp;J. 263, 274-277 (2000) (hereinafter Dreyfuss); Posner, The Law and Economics of Intellectual Property, 131 Daedalus 5 (Spring 2002).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 48" name="FNconcurrence1.48" href="#FRconcurrence1.48">48</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;C. Coolidge, The Press Under a Free Government, in Foundations of the Republic: Speeches and Addresses 187 (1926).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 49" name="FNconcurrence1.49" href="#FRconcurrence1.49">49</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also Pollack 75-76 ("Since business methods are 'useful' when they directly earn revenue, they are inherently unlikely to be underproduced").</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 50" name="FNconcurrence1.50" href="#FRconcurrence1.50">50</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See R. Levin et&nbsp;al., Appropriating the Returns from Industrial Research and Development, in 3 Brookings Papers on Econ. Activity 794-795 (1987).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 51" name="FNconcurrence1.51" href="#FRconcurrence1.51">51</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Burk &amp; Lemley 1618; Dreyfuss 275; see generally Carrier 821-823.  Concededly, there may some methods of doing business that do not confer sufficient first-mover advantages.  See Abramowicz &amp; Duffy, Intellectual Property for Market Experimentation, 83 N.&nbsp;Y. U. L.&nbsp;Rev. 337, 340-342 (2008).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 52" name="FNconcurrence1.52" href="#FRconcurrence1.52">52</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Burk &amp; Lemley 1618; Carrier 826; Olson, Taking the Utilitarian Basis for Patent Law Seriously: The Case For Restricting Patentable Subject Matter, 82 Temp. L.&nbsp;Rev. 181, 231 (2009). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 53" name="FNconcurrence1.53" href="#FRconcurrence1.53">53</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Dreyfuss 276; Merges &amp; Nelson, On the Complex Economics of Patent Scope, 90 Colum. L.&nbsp;Rev. 839, 873-878 (1990).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 54" name="FNconcurrence1.54" href="#FRconcurrence1.54">54</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also Raskind, The <em>State Street Bank</em> Decision, The Bad Business of Unlimited Patent Protection for Methods of Doing Business, 10 Fordham Intell. Prop. Media &amp; Ent. L.&nbsp;J. 61, 102 (1999) ("Interactive emulation more than innovation is the driving force of business method changes").  </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 55" name="FNconcurrence1.55" href="#FRconcurrence1.55">55</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;There is substantial academic debate, moreover, about whether the normal process of screening patents for novelty and obviousness can function effectively for business methods.  The argument goes that because business methods are both vague and not confined to any one industry, there is not a well-confined body of prior art to consult, and therefore many "bad" patents are likely to issue, a problem that would need to be sorted out in later litigation.  See, <em>e.g.</em>, Dreyfuss 268-270; Eisenberg, Analyze This: A Law and Economics Agenda for the Patent System, 53 Vand. L.&nbsp;Rev. 2081, 2090 (2000); Merges 589-590.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 56" name="FNconcurrence1.56" href="#FRconcurrence1.56">56</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See also J. Bessen &amp; M. Meurer, Patent Failure: How Judges, Bureaucrats, and Lawyers Put Innovators at Risk 46-72 (2008) (hereinafter Bessen &amp; Meurer); P. Menell &amp; S. Scotchmer, Intellectual Property Law, in 2 Handbook of Law and Economics 1500-1501, 1506 (M. Polinsky &amp; S. Shavell eds. 2007).  Concededly, alterations in the remedy structure, such as the First Inventor Defense Act of 1999, §4301 <em>et seq., </em>113 Stat. 1536, codified at 35 U.&nbsp;S.&nbsp;C. §273, mitigate these costs.</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 57" name="FNconcurrence1.57" href="#FRconcurrence1.57">57</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See generally Farrell &amp; Shapiro, How Strong Are Weak Patents?  98 Amer. Econ. Rev. 1347 (2008); Meurer, Controlling Opportunistic and Anti-Competitive Intellectual Property Litigation, 44 Boston College L. Rev. 509 (2003); Moore, Populism and Patents, 82 N.&nbsp;Y. U.&nbsp;L. Rev. 69, 90-91 (2007). </p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 58" name="FNconcurrence1.58" href="#FRconcurrence1.58">58</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;See Bessen &amp; Meurer 176; Lessig, The Death of Cyberspace, 57 Wash. &amp; Lee L.&nbsp;Rev. 337, 346-347 (2000).</p>

<br><strong>Footnote <a aria-label="Move to citation 59" name="FNconcurrence1.59" href="#FRconcurrence1.59">59</a></strong><br><p>&nbsp;Congress and the courts have worked long and hard to create and administer antitrust laws that ensure businesses cannot prevent each other from competing vigorously.  If methods of conducting business were themselves patentable, then virtually any novel, nonobvious business method could be granted a federally protected monopoly.  The tension this might create with our antitrust regime provides yet another reason for skepticism that Congress would have wanted the patent laws to extend to business methods.</p>
 
        <p></p>
        <p></p>
    </div>

<div class="caseInformation section">

    <div class="case-information">
        <h2 class="case-information__title"> BILSKI <span style="TEXT-TRANSFORM: UPPERCASE;"><small>ET AL. </small></span><em>v</em>. KAPPOS, UNDER SECRETARY OF COMMERCE FOR INTELLECTUAL PROPERTY AND DIRECTOR, PATENT AND TRADEMARK OFFICE (2010)</h2>
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Docket No:</b> No. 08-964</p>
        
        
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Argued:</b> November 09, 2009</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Decided:</b> June 28, 2010</p>
        <p class="case-information__paragraph"><b class="case-information__paragraph-topic">Court:</b> United States Supreme Court</p>
    </div>

    <h2 class="lawyer-search__search-title">Need to find an attorney?</h2>
    <p class="lawyer-search__search-subtitle">Search our directory by legal issue</p>
    <p class="lawyer-search__form-instructions">Enter information in one or both fields (Required)</p>
    <form class="lawyer-search__form" method="get" action="https://lawyers.findlaw.com/lawyer/lawyer_dir/search/jsp/stdSearch_process.jsp">

_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory = function(nameString, href) {
  // TODO remove this once the correct PA is being used for the lid
  if( _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context') && _satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa ) {
    nameString = nameString.replace(/pa:[0-9]+/, "pa:"+_satellite.getVar('ad_decision_context').pa);  
  }
  _satellite.logger.debug('_satellite.ads.adInfoFactory nameString: '+nameString);

  // sample name: "&lid=clk:fs/prod:dts/cid:5088127/msa:259/pa:1313/sub:3107462/pos:1"
  var s = nameString;
  s = s.substr(5);
  var lid = s;
  s = s.replaceAll(':','":"');
  s = s.replaceAll('/','","');
  s = '{"'+s+'"}'
  var adInfo = JSON.parse(s);
  adInfo.lid = lid;
  adInfo.paid = (typeof adInfo.sub != 'undefined');
  adInfo.adId = (adInfo.paid ? adInfo.sub : adInfo.cid);
  var map = {
    'ph':'phone',
    'fs':'website',
    'pv':'profile',
    'cu':'contact',
    'vr':'review-read',
    'wr':'review-write',
    'sl':'super-lawyers'
  };  
  adInfo.type = map[adInfo.clk];   
  adInfo.transactionId = _satellite.getVar('fl_user_id')+'-'+adInfo.adId+'-'+(new Date().getTime());
  
  if( href && adInfo.type == 'phone' ) {
    adInfo.detail = href.replace(/[^\d]/g,'');
    if( adInfo.detail.length > 10 ) {
      adInfo.detail = adInfo.detail.substr(adInfo.detail.length-10);
    }
  }  
  
  return adInfo;
}

_satellite.ads.trackAdEngagement = function(adInfo) {
  if( typeof adInfo.adId == 'undefined' ) {
    return false;
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads = {};
  }
  if( typeof FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements == 'undefined' ) {
    FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements = [];
  }
  for( var i=0; i < FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.length; i++ ) {
    if( FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].type == adInfo.type && FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements[i].adId == adInfo.adId ) {
      return true;
    }
  }

  FLDataLayer.ads.adEngagements.push(adInfo);
  return false;
}

function setTrackerCookie(name, value)
{
  var hostParts = location.host.split('.').reverse();
  var cookieDomain = '.' + hostParts[1] + '.' + hostParts[0];
  document.cookie = name + '=' + value + ';domain=' + cookieDomain + ';path=/';
}

window.addEventListener("load", function(event) {
  try {

    _satellite.trackSearchSource = function(type){
      let toStore = type;
      if(type.indexOf("Top-Nav")>-1 || type.indexOf("Next Steps")>-1 ){
        const pageType = _satellite.getVar("page_page_type");
        if(pageType == "article") toStore += " Article";
        if(pageType == "index") toStore += " Index";
      }
      var practiceCode = _satellite.getVar("practice_code");
      if (practiceCode) {
        toStore+= "|"+practiceCode;
      }
      _satellite.logger.info('s_dsource',toStore);
      _satellite.writeCrossSubdomainCookie("s_dsource", toStore);
    };

    _satellite.slowDown = function(e, goTo){
      e.preventDefault(); // prevent default anchor behavior
      setTimeout(function() {
        window.location = goTo;
      }, 300);
    }
    
    //CTA Search - enter & click
    const nextStepsForm = document.querySelector('.nextSteps form');
    _satellite.logger.info('nextStepsForm',nextStepsForm);
    if (nextStepsForm) {
          nextStepsForm.addEventListener('submit', function(e) {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Next Steps");
        });
    }


    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - Enter
    const searchComponents = document.querySelectorAll('#search-component,.hero-content form, .search-secondary form');
    _satellite.logger.info('searchComponents',searchComponents);
    searchComponents.forEach((component) => {
      component.addEventListener('keypress', function(e) {
        if (e.key === 'Enter') {
          if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
            _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
            _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
          } else {
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
              _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
          }
        }
      });
    })
    

    //Top Nav & Find a Lawyer Page - click
    const submitButtons = document.querySelectorAll('submit-button, .hero-content input[type="submit"], .search-secondary input[type="submit"]')
    submitButtons.forEach((button) => {
      _satellite.logger.info('button',button);
      button.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
        if (location.host + location.pathname === "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Find a Lawyer");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Find a Lawyer');
        } else {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Top-Nav");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Top-Nav');
        }
      });
    })

    //Homepage search
    if (location.host + location.pathname === "www.findlaw.com/") {

      forms.forEach((form) => {
        _satellite.logger.info('form',form);
        form.addEventListener('submit', function() {
          _satellite.trackSearchSource("Search Box: Home");
          _satellite.logger.info('Search Box','Home');
        });
      });
    }

    //CTA
    const links = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="fli="], .articleHeading p a[href*="lawyers.findlaw.com"]');
    _satellite.logger.info('links',links);
    links.forEach((link) => {

        const goTo = link.getAttribute('href');

        let source = link.dataset.searchLocation || '';
        if (!source) {

            source = 'CTA: Banner';
          } else {
            source = 'CTA: Inline Link';
          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
        }
      });
    });

    //Right Rail
    const sidebarLawyerSearch = document.querySelector('.sidebarLawyerSearch');
    _satellite.logger.info('sidebarLawyerSearch',sidebarLawyerSearch);
    if (sidebarLawyerSearch) {
          sidebarLawyerSearch.addEventListener('click', function(event) {
            if (event.target.getAttribute('href') && event.target.getAttribute('href').includes('lawyers.findlaw.com')) {
              const goTo = event.target.getAttribute('href');
              _satellite.slowDown(event, goTo);
              _satellite.trackSearchSource("Link Module: Right Rail");
            }
          });
    }

    //Browse
    if (location.host + location.pathname == "lawyers.findlaw.com/") {
      const lawyerLinks = document.querySelectorAll('a[href*="/lawyer"]');
      _satellite.logger.info('lawyerLinks',lawyerLinks);
      lawyersLinks.forEach((link) => {
        link.addEventListener('click', function(e) {
          const goTo = this.getAttribute('href');
          _satellite.slowDown(e, goTo);
          let source = "";
          if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statepractice/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: Popular Cities";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/state/") > -1) {
            source = "Browse: State";
          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/statecounty/") > -1) {

          } else if (this.getAttribute("href").indexOf("/lawyer/practice/") > -1) {

          }
          _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
          _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);

      });

        _satellite.logger.info('source',source);
        _satellite.trackSearchSource(source);
      });
    });
